L.&nbsp;Rev. 239, 303-310 (1989).  Courts were expected to clarify the meaning of such texts over time as they applied their terms to specific cases.  See <em>id.,</em> at 309-310; Nelson, Originalism and Interpretive Conventions, 70 U. Chi. L. Rev. 519, 526 (2003).  Although early American courts declined to apply vague or unintelligible statutes as appropriate in individual cases, they did not wholesale invalidate them as unconstitutional delegations of legislative power.  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___, and n.&nbsp;3 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (slip op., at 10-11, and n.&nbsp;3).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I need not resolve these historical questions today, as this case can be decided on narrower grounds.  If the vagueness doctrine has any basis in the original meaning of the Due Process Clause, it must be limited to case-by-case challenges to particular applications of a statute.  That is what early American courts did when they applied the rule of lenity.  See <em>id.</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 10).  And that is how early American courts addressed constitutional challenges to statutes more generally.  See <em>ibid.</em> ("[T]here is good evidence that [antebellum] courts .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. understood judicial review to consist 'of a refusal to give a statute effect as operative law in resolving a case,' a notion quite distinct from our modern practice of '&nbsp;"strik[ing] down" legislation'&nbsp;" (quoting Walsh, Partial Unconstitutionality, 85 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;U. L. Rev. 738, 756 (2010)).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This Court's precedents likewise recognize that, outside the First Amendment context, a challenger must prove that the statute is vague as applied to him.  See <em>Holder</em> v. <em>Humanitarian Law Project</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 18-19 (2010); <em>United States </em>v. <em>Williams</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S. 285, 304 (2008); <em>Maynard</em>, 486 U.&nbsp;S., at 361; <em>Hoffman Estates </em>v. <em>Flipside, Hoffman Estates, Inc.</em>, 455 U.&nbsp;S. 489, 495, and n.&nbsp;7 (1982) (collecting cases).  <em>Johnson </em>did not overrule these precedents.  While <em>Johnson</em> weakened the principle that a facial challenge requires a statute to be vague "in <em>all</em> applications," 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 11) (emphasis added), it did not address whether a statute must be vague as applied to the person challenging it.  That question did not arise because the Court concluded that ACCA's residual clause was vague as applied to the crime at issue there: unlawful possession of a short-barreled shotgun.  See <em>id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 9).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In my view, 16(b) is not vague as applied to respondent.  When respondent committed his burglaries in 2007 and 2009, he was "sufficiently forewarned .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. that the statutory consequence .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is deportation."  <em>De George</em>, 341 U.&nbsp;S., at 232.  At the time, courts had "unanimous[ly]" concluded that residential burglary is a crime of violence, and not "a single opinion .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. ha[d] held that [it] is <em>not</em>."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>M.&nbsp;C.&nbsp;E.</em>, 232 F.&nbsp;3d 1252, 1255-1256 (CA9 2000); see also <em>United States</em> v. <em>Davis</em>, 881 F.&nbsp;2d 973, 976 (CA11 1989) (explaining that treating residential burglary as a crime of violence was "[i]n accord with common law tradition and the settled law of the federal circuits").  Residential burglary "ha[d] been considered a violent offense for hundreds of years .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. because of the potential for mayhem if burglar encounters resident."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Pinto</em>, 875 F.&nbsp;2d 143, 144 (CA7 1989).  The Model Penal Code had recognized that risk, see ALI, Model Penal Code 221.1, Comment 3(c), p. 75 (1980); the Sentencing Commission had recognized that risk; see United States Sentencing Commission, Guidelines Manual 4B1.2(a)(2) (Nov. 2006); and this Court had repeatedly recognized that risk, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>James</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 550 U.&nbsp;S. 192, 203 (2007);<em> Taylor</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 495 U.&nbsp;S. 575, 588 (1990).  In <em>Leocal </em>v.<em> Ashcroft</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (2004), this Court unanimously agreed that burglary is the "classic example" of a crime of violence under 16(b), because it "involves a substantial risk that the burglar will use force against a victim in completing the crime."  <em>Id.</em>, at 10.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That same risk is present with respect to respondent's statute of conviction--first-degree residential burglary, Cal. Penal Code Ann. 459, 460(a) (West 1999).  The California Supreme Court has explained that the State's burglary laws recognize "the dangers to personal safety created by the <em>usual</em> burglary situation."  <em>People</em> v. <em>Davis</em>, 18 Cal. 4th 712, 721, 958 P.&nbsp;2d 1083, 1089 (1998) (emphasis added).  "&nbsp;'[T]he fact that a building is used as a home .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. increases such danger,'&nbsp;" which is why California elevates residential burglary to a first-degree offense.  <em>People</em> v. <em>Rodriguez</em>, 122 Cal. App. 4th 121, 133, 18 Cal.&nbsp;Rptr.&nbsp;3d 550, 558 (2004); see also <em>People </em>v. <em>Wilson</em>, 208 Cal. App.&nbsp;3d 611, 615, 256 Cal. Rptr. 422, 425 (1989) ("[T]he higher degree .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is intended to prevent those situations which are most dangerous, most likely to cause personal injury" (emphasis deleted)).  Although unlawful entry is not an element of the offense, courts "unanimous[ly]" agree that the offense still involves a substantial risk of physical force.  <em>United States </em>v. <em>Avila</em>, 770 F.&nbsp;3d 1100, 1106 (CA4 2014); accord, <em>United States </em>v. <em>Maldonado</em>, 696 F.&nbsp;3d 1095, 1102, 1104 (CA10 2012); <em>United States </em>v. <em>Scanlan</em>, 667 F.&nbsp;3d 896, 900 (CA7 2012); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Echeverria-Gomez</em>, 627 F.&nbsp;3d 971, 976 (CA5 2010); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Becker</em>, 919 F.&nbsp;2d 568, 573 (CA9 1990).  First-degree residential burglary requires entry into an inhabited dwelling, with the intent to commit a felony, against the will of the homeowner--the key elements that create the risk of violence.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Park</em>, 649 F.&nbsp;3d 1175, 1178-1180 (CA9 2011); <em>Avila</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 1106-1107; <em>Becker</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 571, n.&nbsp;5.  As this Court has explained, "[t]he main risk of burglary arises not from the simple physical act of wrongfully entering onto another's property, but rather from the possibility of a face-to-face confrontation between the burglar and a third party."  <em>James</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 203.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Drawing on <em>Johnson</em> and the decision below, the Court suggests that residential burglary might not be a crime of violence because "&nbsp;'only about seven percent of burglaries actually involve violence.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante,</em> at 9, n.&nbsp;3 (citing <em>Dimaya</em> v. <em>Lynch</em>, 803 F.&nbsp;3d 1110, 1116, n.&nbsp;7 (CA9 2015)); see Bureau of Justice Statistics, S. Catalano, National Crime Victimization Survey: Victimization During Household Burglary 1 (Sept. 2010), https://www.bjs.gov/content/pub/ pdf/vdhb.pdf (as last visited Apr. 13, 2018).  But this statistic--which measures actual violence against a member of the household, see <em>id.,</em> at 1, 12--is woefully underinclusive.  It excludes other potential victims besides household members--for example, "a police officer, or a bystande[r] who comes to investigate," <em>James</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 203.  And 16(b) requires only a risk of physical force, not actual physical force, and that risk would seem to be present whenever someone is home during the burglary.  Further, <em>Johnson </em>is not conclusive because, unlike ACCA's residual clause, 16(b) covers offenses that involve a substantial risk of physical force "against the person <em>or property</em> of another."  (Emphasis added.)  Surely the ordinary case of residential burglary involves at least one of these risks.  According to the statistics referenced by the Court, most burglaries involve either a forcible entry (<em>e.g., </em>breaking a window or slashing a door screen), an attempted forcible entry, or an unlawful entry when someone is home.  See Bureau of Justice Statistics, <em>supra,</em> at 2 (Table 1).  Thus, under any metric, respondent's convictions for first-degree residential burglary are crimes of violence under 16(b).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, if facial vagueness challenges are ever appropriate, I adhere to my view that a law is not facially vague "&nbsp;'[i]f any fool would know that a particular category of conduct would be within the reach of the statute, if there is an unmistakable core that a reasonable person would know is forbidden by the law.'&nbsp;"  <em>Morales</em>, 527 U.&nbsp;S., at 112 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., dissenting) (quoting <em>Kolender</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S., at 370-371 (White, J., dissenting)).  The residual clause of ACCA had such a core.  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 10); <em>id.,</em> at ___-___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito,</small></span> J., dissenting) (slip op., at 14-15).  And 16(b) has an even wider core, as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice </small></span>explains.  Thus, the Court should not have invalidated 16(b), either on its face or as applied to respondent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even taking the vagueness doctrine and <em>Johnson </em>at face value, I disagree with the Court's decision to invalidate 16(b).  The sole reason that the Court deems 16(b) unconstitutionally vague is because it reads the statute as incorporating the categorical approach--specifically, the "ordinary case" approach from ACCA's residual clause.  Although the Court mentions "[t]wo features" of 16(b) that make it vague--the ordinary-case approach and an imprecise risk standard--the Court admits that the second feature is problematic only in combination with the first.  <em>Ante,</em> at 8.  Without the ordinary-case approach, the Court "do[es] not doubt" the constitutionality of 16(b).  <em>Ante,</em> at 10.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But if the categorical approach renders 16(b) unconstitutionally vague, then constitutional avoidance requires us to make a reasonable effort to avoid that interpretation.  And a reasonable alternative interpretation is available: Instead of asking whether the ordinary case of an alien's offense presents a substantial risk of physical force, courts should ask whether the alien's actual underlying conduct presents a substantial risk of physical force.  I will briefly discuss the origins of the categorical approach and then explain why the Court should abandon it for 16(b).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The categorical approach originated with Justice Blackmun's opinion for the Court in <em>Taylor</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 495 U.&nbsp;S. 575 (1990).  The question in <em>Taylor</em> was whether ACCA's reference to "burglary" meant burglary as defined by state law or burglary in the generic sense.  After "devoting 10 pages of [its] opinion to legislative history," <em>id.,</em> at 603 (Scalia, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment), and finding that Congress had made "an inadvertent casualty in [the] complex drafting process," <em>id.,</em> at 589-590 (majority opinion), the Court concluded that ACCA referred to burglary in the generic sense, <em>id.,</em> at 598.  The Court then addressed how the Government would prove that a defendant was convicted of generic burglary, as opposed to another offense.  <em>Id.,</em> at 599-602.  <em>Taylor</em> rejected the notion that the Government could introduce evidence about the "particular facts" of the defendant's underlying crime.  <em>Id.,</em> at 600.  Instead, the Court adopted a "categorical approach," which focused primarily on the "statutory definition of the prior offense."  <em>Id.,</em> at 602.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although <em>Taylor </em>was interpreting one of ACCA's enumerated offenses, this Court later extended the categorical approach to ACCA's residual clause.  See <em>James</em>, 550 U.&nbsp;S., at 208.  That extension required some reworking.  Because ACCA's enumerated-offenses clause asks whether a prior conviction "is burglary, arson, or extortion," 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 924(e)(2)(B)(ii), <em>Taylor</em> instructed courts to focus on the definition of the underlying crime.  The residual clause, by contrast, asks whether a prior conviction "involves conduct that presents a serious potential risk of physical injury to another."  924(e)(2)(B)(ii).  Thus, the Court held that the categorical approach for the residual clause asks "whether the conduct encompassed by the elements of the offense, <em>in the ordinary case</em>, presents a serious potential risk of injury to another."  <em>James</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 208 (emphasis added).  This "ordinary case" approach allowed courts to apply the residual clause without inquiring into the individual facts of the defendant's prior crime.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Taylor</em> gave a few reasons why the categorical approach was the correct reading of ACCA, see 495 U.&nbsp;S., at 600-601, but the "heart of the decision" was the Court's concern with limiting the amount of evidence that the parties could introduce at sentencing.  <em>Shepard</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S. 13, 23 (2005).  Specifically, the Court was worried about potential violations of the Sixth Amendment.  If the parties could introduce evidence about the defendant's underlying conduct, then sentencing proceedings might devolve into a full-blown minitrial, with factfinding by the judge instead of the jury.  See <em>id.</em>, at 24-26; <em>Taylor</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 601.  While this Court's decision in <em>Almendarez-Torres</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 523 U.&nbsp;S. 224 (1998), allows judges to find facts about a defendant's prior convictions, a full-blown minitrial would look "too much like" the kind of factfinding that the Sixth Amendment requires the jury to conduct.  <em>Shepard</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S., at 25.  By construing ACCA to require a categorical approach, then, the Court was following "[t]he rule of reading statutes to avoid serious risks of unconstitutionality."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I disagreed with the Court's decision to extend the categorical approach to ACCA's residual clause.  See <em>James</em>, 550 U.&nbsp;S., at 231-232 (dissenting opinion).  The categorical approach was an "&nbsp;'unnecessary exercise,'&nbsp;" I explained, because it created the same Sixth Amendment problem that it tried to avoid.  <em>Id.,</em> at 231.  Absent waiver, a defendant has the right to have a jury find "every fact that is by law a basis for imposing or increasing punishment," including the fact of a prior conviction.  <em>Apprendi</em> v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 466, 501 (2000) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.</small></span>, concurring).  The exception recognized in <em>Almendarez-Torres </em>for prior convictions is an aberration, has been seriously undermined by subsequent precedents, and should be reconsidered.  See <em>Mathis</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2016) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.,</small></span> concurring) (slip op., at 1); <em>Shepard</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 27-28 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.,</small></span> concurring in part and concurring in judgment).  In my view, if the Government wants to enhance a defendant's sentence based on his prior convictions, it must put those convictions in the indictment and prove them to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRopinion1.6" href="#FNopinion1.6">6</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;My objection aside, the ordinary-case approach soon created problems of its own.  The Court's attempt to avoid the Scylla of the Sixth Amendment steered it straight into the Charybdis of the Fifth.  The ordinary-case approach that was created to honor the individual right to a jury is now, according to the Court, so vague that it deprives individuals of due process.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I see no good reason for the Court to persist in reading the ordinary-case approach into 16(b).  The text of 16(b) does not mandate the ordinary-case approach, the concerns that led this Court to adopt it do not apply here, and there are no prudential reasons for retaining it.  In my view, we should abandon the categorical approach for 16(b).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The text of 16(b) does not require a categorical approach.  The INA declares an alien deportable if he is  </p>
<p>"convicted of an aggravated felony" after he is admitted to the United States.  8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii).  Aggravated felonies include "crime[s] of violence" as defined in 16.  1101(a)(43)(F).  Section 16, in turn, defines crimes of violence as follows:</p>
<blockquote><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "(a)an offense that has as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person or property of another, or</p>
</blockquote><blockquote><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "(b)any other offense that is a felony and that, by its nature, involves a substantial risk that physical force against the person or property of another may be used in the course of committing the offense."</p>
</blockquote><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;At first glance, 16(b) is not clear about the precise question it poses.  On the one hand, the statute might refer to the metaphysical "nature" of the offense and ask whether it ordinarily involves a substantial risk of physical force.  On the other hand, the statute might refer to the underlying facts of the offense that the offender committed; the words "by its nature," "substantial risk," and "may" would mean only that an offender who engages in risky conduct cannot benefit from the fortuitous fact that physical force was not actually used during his offense.  The text can bear either interpretation.  See <em>Nijhawan</em> v. <em>Holder</em>, 557 U.&nbsp;S. 29, 33-34 (2009) ("[I]n ordinary speech words such as 'crime,' 'felony,' 'offense,' and the like sometimes refer to a generic crime .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. and sometimes refer to the specific acts in which an offender engaged on a specific occasion").  It is entirely natural to use words like "nature" and "offense" to refer to an offender's actual underlying conduct.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRopinion1.7" href="#FNopinion1.7">7</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although both interpretations are linguistically possible, several factors indicate that the underlying-conduct approach is the better one.  To begin, 16(b) asks whether an offense "involves" a substantial risk of force.  The word "involves" suggests that the offense must <em>necessarily</em> include a substantial risk of force.  See The New Oxford Dictionary of English 962 (2001) ("include (something) as a necessary part or result"); Random House Dictionary of the English Language 1005 (2d ed. 1987) ("1. to include as  </p>
<p>a necessary circumstance, condition, or consequence"); Oxford American Dictionary 349 (1980) ("1. to contain within itself, to make necessary as a condition or result").  That condition is always satisfied if the Government must prove that the alien's underlying conduct involves a substantial risk of force, but it is not always satisfied if the Government need only prove that the "ordinary case" involves such a risk.  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 12).  Tellingly, the other aggravated felonies in the INA that use the word "involves" employ the underlying-conduct approach.  See 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1101(a)(43)(M)(i) ("an offense that involves fraud or deceit in which the loss to the victim or victims exceeds $10,000"); 1101(h)(3) ("any crime of reckless driving or of driving while intoxicated or under the influence of alcohol or of prohibited substances if such crime involves personal injury to another").  As do the similarly worded provisions of the Comprehensive Crime Control Act of 1984, the bill that contained 16(b).  See, <em>e.g.,</em> 98 Stat. 2059 (elevating the burden of proof for the release of "a person found not guilty only by reason of insanity of an offense involving bodily injury to, or serious damage to the property of, another person, or involving a substantial risk of such injury or damage"); <em>id.,</em> at 2068 (establishing the sentence for drug offenses "involving" specific quantities and types of drugs); <em>id., </em>at 2137 (defining violent crimes in aid of racketeering to include "attempting or conspiring to commit a crime involving maiming, assault with a dangerous weapon, or assault resulting in serious bodily injury").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A comparison of 16(b) and 16(a) further highlights why the former likely adopts an underlying-conduct approach.  Section 16(a) covers offenses that have the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force "as an element."  Because 16(b) covers "other" offenses and is separated from 16(a) by the disjunctive word "or," the natural inference is that 16(b) asks a different<em> </em>question.  In other words, 16(b) must require immigration judges to look beyond the elements of an offense to determine whether it involves a substantial risk of physical force.  But if the elements are insufficient, where else should immigration judges look to determine the riskiness of an offense?  Two options are possible, only one of which is workable.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The first option is to consult the underlying facts of the alien's crime and then assess its riskiness.  This approach would provide a definitive answer in every case.  And courts are already familiar with this kind of inquiry.  Cf. <em>Johnson</em>, <em>supra,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 12) (noting that "dozens" of similarly worded laws ask courts to assess "the riskiness of conduct in which an individual defendant engages <em>on a particular occasion</em>").  Nothing suggests that Congress imposed a more limited inquiry when it enacted 16(b) in 1984.  At the time, Congress had not yet enacted ACCA's residual clause, this Court had not yet created the categorical approach, and this Court had not yet recognized a Sixth Amendment limit on judicial factfinding at sentencing, see <em>Chambers </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 122, 132 (2009) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, </small></span>J., concurring in judgment).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The second option is to imagine the "ordinary case" of the alien's crime and then assess the riskiness of that hypothetical offense.  But the phrase "ordinary case" does not appear in the statute.  And imagining the ordinary case, the Court reminds us, is "hopeless[ly] indetermina[te]," "wholly 'speculative,'&nbsp;" and mere "guesswork."  <em>Ante, </em>at 7, 24 (quoting <em>Johnson</em>, <em>supra,</em> at ___-___ (slip op., at 5, 7)); see also <em>Chambers</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 133 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, </small></span>J.) (observing that the categorical approach is "nearly impossible to apply consistently").  Because courts disfavor interpretations that make a statute impossible to apply, see A.&nbsp;Scalia &amp; B.&nbsp;Garner, Reading Law 63 (2012), this Court should reject the ordinary-case approach for 16(b) and adopt the underlying-facts approach instead.  See <em>Johnson</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, </small></span>J., dissenting) (slip op., at 10) ("When another interpretation is ready at hand, why should we assume that Congress gave the clause a meaning that is impossible--or even, exceedingly difficult--to apply").</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That the categorical approach is not the better reading of 16(b) should not be surprising, since the categorical approach was never really about the best reading of the text.  As explained, this Court adopted that approach to avoid a potential Sixth Amendment problem with sentencing judges conducting minitrials to determine a defendant's past conduct.  But even assuming the categorical approach solved this Sixth Amendment problem in criminal cases, no such problem arises in immigration cases.  "[T]he provisions of the Constitution securing the right of trial by jury have no application" in a removal proceeding.  <em>Turner</em>, 194 U.&nbsp;S., at 290.  And, in criminal cases, the underlying-conduct approach would be perfectly constitutional if the Government included the defendant's prior conduct in the indictment, tried it to a jury, and proved it beyond a reasonable doubt.  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 12).  Nothing in 16(b) prohibits the Government from proceeding this way, so the plurality is wrong to suggest that the underlying-conduct approach would necessarily "ping-pong us from one constitutional issue to another."  <em>Ante,</em> at 14.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If constitutional avoidance applies here at all, it requires us to <em>reject </em>the categorical approach for 16(b).  According to the Court, the categorical approach is unconstitutionally vague.  And, all agree that the underlying-conduct approach would not be.  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (majority opinion) (slip op., at 12) ("[W]e do not doubt the constitutionality of laws that call for the application of a qualitative standard such as 'substantial risk' to real-world conduct").  Thus, if the underlying-conduct approach is a "reasonabl[e]" interpretation of 16(b), it is our "plain duty" to adopt it.  <em>United States ex rel. Attorney General</em> v. <em>Delaware &amp; Hudson Co.</em>, 213 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 407 (1909).  And it is reasonable, as explained above.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Johnson</em>, the Court declined to adopt the underlying-conduct approach for ACCA's residual clause.  See 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 12-13).  The Court concluded that the categorical approach was the only reasonable reading of ACCA because the residual clause uses the word "convictions."  <em>Id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 13).  The Court also stressed the "utter impracticability of requiring a sentencing court to reconstruct, long after the original conviction, the conduct underlying that conviction."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Neither of these arguments is persuasive with respect to the INA.  Moreover, this Court has already rejected them.  In <em>Nijhawan</em>, this Court unanimously concluded that one of the aggravated felonies in the INA--"an offense that .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. involves fraud or deceit in which the loss to the victim or victims exceeds $10,000," 1101(a)(43)(M)(i)--applies the underlying-conduct approach, not the categorical approach.  557 U.&nbsp;S., at 32.  Although the INA also refers to "convict[ions]," 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), the Court was not swayed by that argument.  The word "convict[ion]" means only that the defendant's underlying conduct must "&nbsp;'be tied to the specific counts covered by the conviction,'&nbsp;" not "acquitted or dismissed counts or general conduct."  <em>Id.,</em> at 42.  As for the supposed practical problems with proving an alien's prior conduct, the Court did not find that argument persuasive either.  "[T]he 'sole purpose' of the 'aggravated felony' inquiry," the Court explained, "&nbsp;'is to ascertain the nature of a prior conviction; it is not an invitation to relitigate the conviction itself.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ibid.</em>  And because the INA places the burden on the Government to prove an alien's conduct by clear and convincing evidence, 1229a(c)(3)(A), "uncertainties caused by the passage of time are likely to count in the alien's favor," <em>id.</em>, at 42.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There are additional reasons why the practical problems identified in <em>Johnson </em>should not matter for 16(b)--even assuming they should have mattered for ACCA's residual clause, see <em>Lewis</em> v. <em>Chicago</em>, 560 U.&nbsp;S. 205, 217 (2010) ("[I]t is not our task to assess the consequences of each approach and adopt the one that produces the least mischief.  Our charge is to give effect to the law Congress enacted").  In a removal proceeding, any difficulties with identifying an alien's past conduct will fall on immigration judges, not federal courts.  But those judges are already accustomed to finding facts about the conduct underlying an alien's prior convictions, since some of the INA's aggravated felonies employ the underlying-conduct approach.  The BIA has instructed immigration judges to determine such conduct based on "any evidence admissible in removal proceedings," not just the elements of the offense or the record of conviction.  See <em>Matter of Babaisakov</em>, 24 I. &amp; N. Dec. 306, 307 (2007).  No one has submitted any evidence that the BIA's approach has been "utter[ly] impracticab[le]" or "daunting[ly] difficul[t]" in practice.  <em>Ante,</em> at 15.  And even if it were, "how much time the agency wants to devote to the resolution of particular issues is .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. a question for the agency itself."  <em>Ali</em> v. <em>Mukasey</em>, 521 F.&nbsp;3d 737, 741 (CA7 2008).  Hypothetical burdens on the BIA should not influence how this Court interprets 16(b).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In short, we should not blithely assume that the reasons why this Court adopted the categorical approach for ACCA's residual clause also apply to the INA's list of aggravated felonies.  As <em>Nijhawan</em> explained, "the 'aggravated felony' statute, unlike ACCA, contains some language that refers to generic crimes and some language that almost certainly refers to the specific circumstances in which a crime was committed."  557 U.&nbsp;S., at 38.  "The question" in each case is "to which category [the aggravated felony] belongs."  <em>Ibid.</em>  As I have explained, 16(b) belongs in the underlying-conduct category.  Because that is the better reading of 16(b)'s text--or at least a reasonable reading--the Court should have adopted it here.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I see no prudential reason for maintaining the categorical approach for 16(b).  The Court notes that the Government "explicitly acknowledges" that 16(b) employs the categorical approach.  <em>Ante,</em> at 9.  But we cannot permit the Government's concessions to dictate how we interpret a statute, much less cause us to invalidate a statute enacted by a coordinate branch.  See <em>United States Nat. Bank of Ore.</em> v. <em>Independent Ins. Agents of America, Inc.</em>, 508 U.&nbsp;S. 439, 446-447 (1993); <em>Young</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 315 U.&nbsp;S. 257, 258-259 (1942).  This Court's "traditional practice" is to "refus[e] to decide constitutional questions" when other grounds of decision are available, "whether or not they have been properly raised before us by the parties."  <em>Neese</em> v. <em>Southern R. Co.</em>, 350 U.&nbsp;S. 77, 78 (1955) (<em>per curiam</em>); see also Vermeule, Saving Constructions, 85 Geo. L.&nbsp;J. 1945, 1948-1949 (1997) (explaining that courts commonly "decide an antecedent statutory issue, even one waived by the parties, if its resolution could preclude a constitutional claim").  This Court has raised potential saving constructions "on our own motion" when they could avoid a ruling on constitutional vagueness grounds, even in cases where the Government was a party.  <em>United States</em> v. <em>L. Cohen Grocery Co.</em>, 255 U.&nbsp;S. 81, 88 (1921).  We should have followed that established practice here.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor should <em>stare decisis</em> prevent us from rejecting the categorical approach for 16(b).  This Court has never held that 16(b) incorporates the ordinary-case approach.  Although <em>Leocal</em> held that 16(b) incorporates a version of the categorical approach, the Court must not feel bound by that decision, as it largely overrules it today.  See <em>ante,</em> at 22, n.&nbsp;7.  Surely the Court cannot credibly invoke <em>stare decisis</em> to defend the categorical approach--the same approach it says only a "lunatic" would continue to apply.  <em>Ante, </em>at 24.  If the Court views the categorical approach that way--the same way <em>Johnson </em>viewed it--then it must also agree that "[s]tanding by [the categorical approach] would undermine, rather than promote, the goals that <em>stare decisis</em> is meant to serve."  576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 15).  That is especially true if the Court's decision leads to the invalidation of scores of similarly worded state and federal statutes, which seems even more likely after today than it did after <em>Johnson</em>.  Instead of adhering to an interpretation that it thinks unconstitutional and then using that interpretation to strike down another statute, the Court should have taken this opportunity to abandon the categorical approach for 16(b) once and for all.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">***</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's decision today is triply flawed.  It unnecessarily extends our incorrect decision in <em>Johnson</em>.  It uses a constitutional doctrine with dubious origins to invalidate yet another statute (while calling into question countless more).  And it does all this in the name of a statutory interpretation that we should have discarded long ago.  Because I cannot follow the Court down any of these rabbit holes, I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The analysis thus differs from the form of categorical approach used to determine whether a prior conviction is for a particular listed offense (say, murder or arson).  In that context, courts ask what the elements of a given crime always require--in effect, what is legally necessary for a conviction.  See, <em>e.g., Descamps </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S. 254, 260-261 (2013); <em>Moncrieffe </em>v. <em>Holder</em>, 569 U.&nbsp;S. 184, 190-191 (2013).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>Shuti</em> v. <em>Lynch</em>, 828 F. 3d 440 (CA6 2016) (finding 16(b) unconstitutionally vague); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Vivas-Ceja</em>, 808 F.&nbsp;3d 719 (CA7 2015) (same), with <em>United States</em> v. <em>Gonzalez-Longoria</em>, 831 F.&nbsp;3d 670 (CA5 2016) (en banc) (upholding 16(b)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Johnson</em> also anticipated and rejected a significant aspect of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span>'s dissent in this case.  According to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span>, a court may not invalidate a statute for vagueness if it is clear in any of its applications--as he thinks is true of <em>completed</em> burglary, which is the offense Dimaya committed.  See <em>post, </em>at 16-20.  But as an initial matter, <em>Johnson </em>explained that supposedly easy applications of the residual clause might not be "so easy after all."  576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 10-11).  The crime of completed burglary at issue here illustrates that point forcefully.  See <em>id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 6) (asking whether "an ordinary burglar invade[s] an occupied home by night or an unoccupied home by day"); <em>Dimaya </em>v. <em>Lynch, </em>803 F.&nbsp;3d 1110, 1116, n.&nbsp;7 (CA9 2015) (noting that only about seven percent of burglaries actually involve violence); Cal. Penal Code Ann. 459, 460 (West 2010) (sweeping so broadly as to cover even dishonest door-to-door salesmen).  And still more fundamentally, <em>Johnson </em>made clear that our decisions "squarely contradict the theory that a vague provision is constitutional merely because there is some conduct that clearly falls within the provision's grasp."  576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 11).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>'s dissent makes light of the difficulty of identifying a crime's ordinary case.  In a single footnote, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> portrays that task as no big deal: Just eliminate the "atypical" cases, and (presto!) the crime's nature and risk are revealed.  See <em>post, </em>at 5, n.&nbsp;1.  That rosy view--at complete odds with <em>Johnson</em>--underlies his whole dissent (and especially, his analysis of how 16(b) applies to particular offenses, see <em>post, </em>at 7-10).  In effect, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice </small></span>is able to conclude that 16(b) can survive <em>Johnson </em>only by refusing to acknowledge one of the two core insights of that decision.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For example, in <em>United States</em> v. <em>Hayes</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 415 (2009), this Court held that a firearms statute referring to former crimes as "committed by" specified persons requires courts to consider underlying facts.  <em>Id.,</em> at 421.  And in <em>Nijhawan</em> v. <em>Holder</em>, 557 U.&nbsp;S. 29 (2009), the Court similarly adopted a non-categorical interpretation of one of the aggravated felonies listed in the INA because of the phrase, appended to the named offense, "in which the loss to the victim or victims exceeds $10,000."  <em>Id.,</em> at 34, 36 (emphasis deleted).  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> suggests that <em>Nijhawan </em>rejected the relevance of our ACCA precedents in interpreting the INA's aggravated-felony list--including its incorporation of 16(b).  <em>Post, </em>at 29-30.  But that misreads the decision.  In <em>Nijhawan</em>, we considered an item on the INA's list that looks nothing like ACCA, and we concluded--no surprise here--that our ACCA decisions did not offer a useful guide.  As to items on the INA's list that <em>do </em>mirror ACCA, the opposite conclusion of course follows.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In response to repeated questioning at two oral arguments, the Government proposed one (and only one) such crime--but we disagree that 16(b)'s temporal language would aid in its analysis.  According to the Government, possession of a short-barreled shotgun could count as violent under ACCA but not under 16(b) because shooting the gun is "not in the course of committing the crime of possession."  Tr. of Oral Arg. 59-60 (Oct. 2, 2017); see Tr. of Oral Arg. 6-7 (Jan. 17, 2017); Brief for Petitioner 32-34.  That is just wrong: When a criminal shoots a gun, he does so while ("in the course of&nbsp;") possessing it (except perhaps in some physics-defying fantasy world).  What makes the offense difficult to classify as violent is something different: that while some people use the short-barreled shotguns they possess to commit murder, others merely store them in a nearby firearms cabinet--and it is hard to settle which is the more likely scenario.  Compare <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 19-20) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, J.</small></span>, dissenting) ("It is fanciful to assume that a person who [unlawfully possesses] a notoriously dangerous weapon is unlikely to use that weapon in violent ways"), with <em>id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 4) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J., </small></span>concurring) (Unlawful possession of a short-barreled shotgun "takes place in a variety of ways .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. many, perhaps most, of which do not involve likely accompanying violence" (internal quotation marks omitted)).  But contrary to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>'s suggestion, see <em>post, </em>at 7-8 (which, again, is tied to his disregard of the ordinary-case inquiry, see <em>supra,</em> at 10, n. 4), that issue must be settled no less under 16(b) than under ACCA.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;And, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> emphasizes, we decided that one unanimously!  See <em>post, </em>at 3 (discussing <em>Leocal</em> v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (2004)).  But one simple application does not a clear statute make.  As we put the point in <em>Johnson</em>: Our decisions "squarely contradict the theory that a vague provision is constitutional merely because there is some conduct that clearly falls within the provision's grasp."  576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 11); see <em>supra,</em> at 9, n.&nbsp;4.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>Escudero-Arciniega </em>v.<em> Holder</em>, 702 F.&nbsp;3d 781, 784-785 (CA5 2012) (<em>per curiam</em>) (yes, it does), and <em>United States </em>v.<em> Guzman-Landeros</em>, 207 F.&nbsp;3d 1034, 1035 (CA8 2000) (<em>per curiam</em>) (same), with <em>Sareang Ye </em>v.<em> INS</em>, 214 F.&nbsp;3d 1128, 1133-1134 (CA9 2000) (no, it does not).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>Aguiar </em>v.<em> Gonzales</em>, 438 F.&nbsp;3d 86, 89-90 (CA1 2006) (statutory rape involves a substantial risk of force); <em>Chery </em>v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, 347 F.&nbsp;3d 404, 408-409 (CA2 2003) (same); and <em>United States </em>v.<em> Velazquez-Overa</em>, 100 F.&nbsp;3d 418, 422 (CA5 1996) (same), with <em>Valencia </em>v.<em> Gonzales</em>, 439 F.&nbsp;3d 1046, 1052 (CA9 2006) (statutory rape does not involve such a risk).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>Dixon </em>v.<em> Attorney Gen.</em>, 768 F.&nbsp;3d 1339, 1343-1346 (CA11 2014) (holding that one such statute falls under 16(b)), with <em>Flores-Lopez </em>v.<em> Holder</em>, 685 F.&nbsp;3d 857, 863-865 (CA9 2012) (holding that another does not).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>United States</em> v. <em>Venegas-Ornelas</em>, 348 F.&nbsp;3d 1273, 1277-1278 (CA10 2003) (residential trespass is a crime of violence), with <em>Zivkovic</em> v. <em>Holder</em>, 724 F.&nbsp;3d 894, 906 (CA7 2013) (it is not).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;From all we can tell--and all the Government has told us, see Brief for Petitioner 45-52--lower courts have also decided many fewer cases involving 16(b) than ACCA's residual clause.  That disparity likely reflects the Government's lesser need to rely on 16(b).  That provision is mainly employed (as here) in the immigration context, to establish an "aggravated felony" requiring deportation.  See <em>supra,</em> at 2.  But immigration law offers many other ways to achieve that result.  The INA lists 80 or so crimes that count as aggravated felonies; only if a conviction is not for one of those specified offenses need the Government resort to 16(b) (or another catch-all provision).  See <em>Luna Torres </em>v. <em>Lynch</em>, 578 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2016) (slip op., at 2).  By contrast, ACCA enumerates only four crimes as a basis for enhancing sentences; the Government therefore had reason to use the statute's residual clause more often.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g., Amendariz-Moreno</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 1133 (2009) (vacating and remanding for reconsideration in light of <em>Begay</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S. 137 (2008), and <em>Chambers</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 122 (2009)); <em>Castillo-Lucio</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 1133 (2009) (same); <em>Addo</em> v. <em>Mukasey</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 1132 (2009) (vacating and remanding in light of <em>Chambers</em>); <em>Serna-Guerra</em> v. <em>Holder</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S 1279 (2009) (same); <em>Reyes-Figueroa</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 1132 (2009) (same).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Many state courts also held vague laws ineffectual.  See, <em>e.g., State </em>v. <em>Mann</em>, 2 Ore. 238, 240-241 (1867) (holding statute that prohibited "gambling devices" was "void" because "the term has no settled and definite meaning"); <em>Drake</em> v. <em>Drake</em>, 15 N.&nbsp;C. 110, 115 (1833) (explaining that "if the terms in which [a statute] is couched be so vague as to convey no definite meaning to those whose duty it is to execute it . . . it is necessarily inoperative"); <em>McConvill</em> v. <em>Mayor and Aldermen of Jersey City</em>, 39 N. J. L. 38, 44 (1876) (holding that an ordinance was "bad for vagueness and uncertainty in the thing forbidden"); <em>State</em> v. <em>Boon</em>, 1 N.&nbsp;C. 103, 105 (1801) (refusing to apply a statute because "no punishment whatever can be inflicted; without using a discretion and indulging a latitude, which in criminal cases ought never to be allowed a Judge");<em> Ex parte Jackson</em>, 45 Ark. 158, 164 (1885) (declaring a statutory prohibition on acts "injurious to the public morals" to be "vague" and "simply null" (emphasis deleted)); <em>McJunkins</em> v. <em>State</em>, 10 Ind. 140, 145 (1858) ("It would therefore appear that the term <em>public indecency</em> has no fixed legal meaning--is vague and indefinite, and cannot in itself imply a definite offense"); <em>Jennings</em> v. <em>State</em>, 16 Ind. 335, 336 (1861) ("We are of opinion that for want of a proper definition, no act is made criminal by the terms 'public indecency,' employed in the statute"); <em>Commonwealth</em> v. <em>Bank of Pennsylvania</em>, 3 Watts &amp; Serg. 173, 177 (Pa. 1842) (holding "the language of [shareholder election] legislation so devoid of certainty" that "no valid election [could have] been held, and that none can be held without further legislation"); <em>Cheezem</em> v. <em>State</em>, 2 Ind. 149, 150 (1850) (finding statute to "contai[n] no prohibition of any kind whatever" and thus declaring it "a nullity"); see also Note, Statutory Standards of Personal Conduct: Indefiniteness and Uncertainty as Violations of Due Process, 38 Harv. L. Rev. 963, 964, n.&nbsp;4 (1925) (collecting cases).&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> Virginia Resolutions in 4 Debates on the Federal Constitution 528 (J. Elliot ed. 1836) (explaining that the Act, "by uniting legislative and judicial powers to those of executive, subverts .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the particular organization, and positive provisions of the federal constitution"); Madison's Report on the Virginia Resolutions (Jan. 7, 1800) in 17 Papers of James Madison 318 (D. Mattern ed. 1991) (Madison's Report) (contending that the Act violated "the only preventive justice known to American jurisprudence," because "[t]he ground of suspicion is to be judged of, not by any judicial authority, but by the executive magistrate alone");<em> </em>L.&nbsp;Canfield &amp; H. Wilder, The Making of Modern America 158 (H. Anderson et&nbsp;al. eds. 1952) ("People all over the country protested against the Alien and Sedition Acts"); M. Baseler, "Asylum for Mankind": America, 1607-1800, p. 287 (1998) ("The election of 1800 was a referendum on--and a repudiation of--the Federalist 'doctrines' enunciated in the debates" over, among other things, the Alien Friends Act); Moore, Aliens and the Constitution, 88 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;U. L.&nbsp;Rev. 801, 865, n.&nbsp;300 (2013) ("The Aliens Act and Sedition Act were met with widespread criticism"); Lindsay, Immigration, Sovereignty, and the Constitution of Foreignness, 45 Conn. L.&nbsp;Rev. 743, 759 (2013) ("[T]he [Alien Friends] Act proved wildly unpopular among the American public, and contributed to the Republican electoral triumph in 1800 and the subsequent demise of the Federalist Party").  Whether the law was unenforced or, at most, enforced only once, the literature is not quite clear.  Compare Sidak, War, Liberty, and Enemy Aliens, 67 N. Y. U. L. Rev. 1402, 1406 (1992) (explaining the Act was never enforced); Cole, Enemy Aliens, 54 Stan. L.&nbsp;Rev. 953, 989 (2002) (same); Klein &amp; Wittes, Preventative Detention in American Theory and Practice, 2 Harv. Nat'l Sec. J. 85, 102, n.&nbsp;71 (2011) (same); Rosenfeld, Deportation Proceedings and Due Process of Law, 26 Colum. Hum. Rts. L.&nbsp;Rev. 713, 726, 733 (1995) (same); with Fehlings, Storm on the Constitution: The First Deportation Law, 10 Tulsa J. Comp. &amp; Int'l L. 63, 109 (2002) (stating that the Act was enforced once, on someone who was planning on leaving the country in a few months anyway).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This Court already and long ago held that due process requires affording aliens the "opportunity, at some time, to be heard" before some lawful authority in advance of removal--and it's unclear how that opportunity might be meaningful without fair notice of the law's demands.  <em>The Japanese Immigrant Case</em>, 189 U.&nbsp;S. 86, 101 (1903).  Nor do the cases <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> cites hold that a statutory right to lawful permanent residency in this country can be withdrawn without due process.  <em>Post, </em>at 11 (dissenting opinion).  Rather, each merely holds that the particular statutory removal procedures under attack comported with due process.  See <em>Harisiades</em> v. <em>Shaughnessy</em>, 342 U.&nbsp;S. 580, 584-585 (1952) (rejecting argument that an "alien is entitled to constitutional [due process] protection .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. <em>to the same extent as the citizen</em>" before removal (emphasis added)); <em>United States ex rel. Turner</em> v. <em>Williams</em>, 194 U.&nbsp;S. 279, 289-290 (1904) (deporting an alien found to be in violation of a constitutionally valid law doesn't violate due process); <em>Fong Yue Ting</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 149 U.&nbsp;S. 698, 730 (1893) (deporting an alien who hasn't "complied with the conditions" required to stay in the country doesn't violate due process).<strong>  </strong>Even when it came to judicially unenforceable privileges in the past, "executive officials had to respect statutory privileges that had been granted to private individuals and that Congress had not authorized the officials to abrogate."  Nelson, Adjudication in the Political Branches, 107 Colum. L. Rev. 559, 581 (2007) (emphasis deleted).  So in a case like ours it would've been incumbent on any executive official to determine that the alien committed a qualifying crime and statutory vagueness could pose a disabling problem even there.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;All this "ordinary case" caveat means is that while "[o]ne can always hypothesize unusual cases in which even a prototypically violent crime might not present a genuine risk," courts should exclude those atypical cases in assessing whether the offense qualifies.  <em>James</em>, 550 U.&nbsp;S., at 208.  As we have explained, under that approach, it is not the case that "every conceivable factual offense covered by a statute" must pose the requisite risk "before the offense can be deemed" a crime of violence.  <em>Ibid.  </em>But the same is true of the categorical approach generally.  See <em>ibid.</em> (using the terms just quoted to characterize both the ordinary case approach and the categorical approach for enumerated offenses set forth in <em>Taylor</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 495 U.&nbsp;S. 575 (1990)); <em>Moncrieffe</em> v. <em>Holder</em>, 569 U.&nbsp;S. 184, 191 (2013); <em>Gonzales</em> v. <em>Duenas-Alvarez</em>, 549 U.&nbsp;S. 183, 193 (2007).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court protests that this straightforward analysis fails to take account of the crime's ordinary case.  <em>Ante,</em> at 18-19, n.&nbsp;6.  But the fact that the element of "possession" may "take[ ] place in a variety of ways"--for instance, one may possess a firearm "in a closet, in a storeroom, in a car, in a pocket," "unloaded, disassembled, or locked away," <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>___</small></span> (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment) (slip op., at 4)--matters very little.  That is because none of the alternative ways of satisfying that element produce a substantial risk that the possessor will use physical force against the person or property of another.  And no one would say that a person "possesses" a gun by firing it or threatening someone with it.  Cf. <em>id.,</em> at ___ (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (slip op., at 5) ("[T]he risk that the Government identifies arises not from the act of possessing the weapon, but from the act of using it.").  The Court's insistence that this offense is nonetheless "difficult to classify" under 16(b), <em>ante,</em> at 18, n.&nbsp;6, is surprising in light of our assessment, just two Terms ago, that 16 does not cover "felon-in-possession laws and other firearms offenses," <em>Luna Torres</em> v. <em>Lynch</em>, 578 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2016) (slip op., at 13).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To name a round dozen: <em>Ayestas</em> v. <em>Davis</em>, 584 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2018) (slip op., at 16); <em>Life Technologies Corp.</em> v. <em>Promega Corp.</em>, 580 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2017) (slip op., at 5-8); <em>Virginia</em> v. <em>Hicks</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 113, 119-120, 122-124 (2003); <em>Toyota Motor Mfg., Ky., Inc.</em> v. <em>Williams</em>, 534 U.&nbsp;S. 184, 196-198 (2002); <em>Slack</em> v. <em>McDaniel</em>, 529 U.&nbsp;S. 473, 483-484 (2000); <em>Gentile</em> v. <em>State Bar of Nev.</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 1030, 1075-1076 (1991); <em>Cage</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 498 U.&nbsp;S. 39, 41 (1990) (<em>per curiam</em>); <em>Steadman</em> v. <em>SEC</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S. 91, 98 (1981); <em>Palermo</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 360 U.&nbsp;S. 343, 351-353 (1959); <em>United States</em> v. <em>E. I. du Pont de Nemours &amp; Co.</em>, 353 U.&nbsp;S. 586, 593-596 (1957); <em>Levinson</em> v. <em>Spector Motor Service</em>, 330 U.&nbsp;S. 649, 670-671 (1947); <em>Consolidated Edison Co.</em> v. <em>NLRB</em>, 305 U.&nbsp;S. 197, 229 (1938).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court also finds it probative that "a host of issues" respecting 16(b) "divide" the lower courts.  <em>Ante,</em> at 22.  Yet the Court does little to explain how those alleged conflicts vindicate its particular concern about the provision (namely, the ordinary case inquiry).  And as the Government illustrates, many of those divergent results likely can be chalked up to material differences in the state offense statutes at issue.  Compare <em>Escudero-Arciniega </em>v. <em>Holder</em>, 702 F.&nbsp;3d 781, 783-785 (CA5 2012) (<em>per curiam</em>) (reasoning that New Mexico car burglary "requires that the criminal lack authorization to enter the vehicle--a requirement alone which will most often ensure some force [against property] is used"), with <em>Sareang Ye </em>v. <em>INS</em>, 214 F.&nbsp;3d 1128, 1134 (CA9 2000) (finding it relevant that California car burglary does not require unlawful or unprivileged entry); see Reply Brief 17-20, and nn.&nbsp;5-6.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>In re Winship</em>, 397 U.&nbsp;S. 358, 382-384 (1970) (Black, J., dissenting); Rosenkranz, The Objects of the Constitution, 63 Stan. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1005, 1041-1043 (2011); Berger, "Law of the Land" Reconsidered, 74 Nw. U. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1, 2-17 (1979); Corwin, The Doctrine of Due Process of Law Before the Civil War, 24 Harv. L. Rev. 366, 368-373 (1911); see also 4&nbsp;The Papers of Alexander Hamilton 35 (Syrett &amp; Cooke eds. 1962) ("The words 'due process' have a precise technical import, and .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. can never be referred to an act of legislature").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Before the 19th century, when virtually all felonies were punishable by death, English courts would sometimes go to extremes to find a reason to invoke the rule of lenity.  See Hall, Strict or Liberal Construction of Penal Statutes, 48 Harv. L.&nbsp;Rev. 748, 751 (1935); <em>e.g., ante,</em> at 4-7 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch, J.,</small></span> concurring in part and concurring in judgment) (citing Blackstone's discussion of a case about "cattle").  As the death penalty became less common, courts on this side of the Atlantic tempered the rule of lenity, clarifying that the rule requires an "ambiguity" in the text and cannot be used "to defeat the obvious intention of the legislature."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Wiltberger</em>, 5 Wheat. 76 (1820) (Marshall, C.&nbsp;J.).</p>
<p>Early American courts also declined to apply nonpenal statutes that were "unintelligible."  <em>Johnson </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___, n.&nbsp;3 (2014) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment) (slip op., at 10, n.&nbsp;3); <em>e.g., ante, </em>at 5-6, and n. 1 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J.) (collecting cases).  Like lenity, however, this practice reflected a principle of statutory construction that was much narrower than the modern constitutional vagueness doctrine.  Unintelligible statutes were considered inoperative because they were impossible to apply to individual cases, not because they were unconstitutional for failing to provide "fair notice."  See <em>Johnson</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___, n.&nbsp;3 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, </small></span>J.) (slip op., at 10, n.&nbsp;3).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This distinction between penal and nonpenal statutes would be decisive here because, traditionally, civil deportation laws were not considered penal.  See <em>Bugajewitz</em> v. <em>Adams</em>, 228 U.&nbsp;S. 585, 591 (1913); <em>Fong Yue Ting</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 149 U.&nbsp;S. 698, 709, 730 (1893).  Although this Court has applied a kind of strict construction to civil deportation laws, that practice did not emerge until the mid-20th century.  See <em>Fong Haw Tan</em> v. <em>Phelan</em>, 333 U.&nbsp;S. 6, 10 (1948).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Jeffersonian Democratic-Republicans who opposed the Alien Friends Act primarily represented slave States, and their party's political strength came from the South.  See Fehlings, Storm on the Constitution: The First Deportation Law, 10 Tulsa J. Comp. &amp; Int'l L. 63, 84 (2002).  The Jeffersonians opposed any federal control over immigration, which their constituents feared would be used to pre-empt State laws that prohibited the entry of free blacks.  <em>Id.,</em> at 84-85; see also Berns, Freedom of the Press and the Alien and Sedition Laws: A Reappraisal, 1970 S. Ct. Rev. 109, 116 ("Whether pro- or anti-slavery, most southerners, including Jefferson and Madison .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. were united behind a policy of denying to the national government any competence to deal with the question of slavery").  The fear was that "mobile free Negroes would intermingle with slaves, encourage them to run away, and foment insurrection."  I. Berlin, Slaves Without Masters 92 (1974).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Jeffersonians also argued that the Alien Friends Act violated due process because, if aliens disobeyed the President's orders to leave the country, they could be convicted of a crime and imprisoned without a trial.  See, <em>e.g., </em>Kentucky Resolutions 6, 4 Elliot's Debates 541.  That charge was false.  The Alien Friends Act gave federal courts jurisdiction over alleged violations of the President's orders.  See 4, 1 Stat. 571.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Sixth Amendment is, thus, not a reason to maintain the categorical approach in criminal cases.  Contra, <em>ante,</em> at 13-14 (plurality opinion).  Even if it were, the Sixth Amendment does not apply in immigration cases like this one.  See Part II-B-2, <em>infra</em>.  The plurality contends that, if it must contort the text of 16(b) to avoid a Sixth Amendment problem in criminal cases, then it must also contort the text of 16(b) in immigration cases, even though the Sixth Amendment problem does not arise in the immigration context.  See <em>ante,</em> at 13-14, 15.  But, as I have explained elsewhere, this "lowest common denominator" approach to constitutional avoidance is both ahistorical and illogical.  See <em>Clark</em> v. <em>Martinez</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 371, 395-401 (2005) (dissenting opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> 18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 3553(a)(2) (directing sentencing judges to consider "the nature and circumstances of the offense"); <em>Schware</em> v. <em>Board of Bar Examiners of N. M.</em>, 353 U.&nbsp;S. 232, 242-243 (1957) (describing "the nature of the offense" committed by a bar applicant as "recruiting persons to go overseas to aid the Loyalists in the Spanish Civil War"); <em>TXO Production Corp.</em> v. <em>Alliance Resources Corp.</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S. 443, 482 (1993) (O'Connor, J., dissenting) (describing "the nature of the offense at issue" as not "involving grave physical injury" but rather as a "business dispute between two companies in the oil and gas industry"); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Broce</em>, 488 U.&nbsp;S. 563, 585-587 (1989) (Blackmun, J., dissenting) (describing "the nature of the charged offense" in terms of the specific facts alleged in the indictment); <em>People </em>v. <em>Golba</em>, 273 Mich. App. 603, 611, 729 N.&nbsp;W.&nbsp;2d 916, 922 (2007) ("[T]he underlying factual basis for a conviction governs whether the offense 'by its nature constitutes a sexual offense against an individual who is less than 18 years of age.'&nbsp;" (quoting Mich. Comp. Laws 28.722(e)(xi) (2006))); A Fix for Animal Abusers, Boston Herald, Nov. 22, 2017, p.&nbsp;16 ("prosecutors were so horrified at the nature of his offense--his torture of a neighbor's dog"); P. Ward, Attorney of Convicted Ex-Official Accuses Case's Judge, Pittsburgh Post-Gazette, Nov. 10, 2015, p.&nbsp;B1 (identifying the "nature of his offense" as "taking money from an elderly, widowed client, and giving it to campaign funds"); Cross-Burning-Article Painted an Inaccurate Picture of Young Man in Question, Seattle Times, Aug. 12, 1991, p.&nbsp;A9 ("[The defendant] took no steps to prevent the cross that was burned from being constructed on his family's premises and later .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. assisted in concealing a second cross .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  This was the nature of his offense"); N. Libman, A Parole/Probation Officer Talks With Norma Libman, Chicago Tribune, May 29, 1988,  p.&nbsp;I31 (describing "the nature of the offense" as "not serious" if "there was no definitive threat on life" or if "the dollar- and cents- amount was not great"); E. Walsh, District-U.&nbsp;S. Argument Delays Warrant for Escapee's Arrest, Washington Post, May 29, 1986, p.&nbsp;C1 (describing "the nature of Murray's alleged offenses" as "point[ing] at two officers a gun that was later found to contain one round of ammunition").</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> SESSIONS, ATTORNEY GENERAL<em> v. DIMAYA</em> (2018)</h2>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })

      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });

    //Homepage search

        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);

          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA

            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {

          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {

          }

          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);

    //data-search-location

    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);

      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
